<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Work (physics)</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Mechanical_work&amp;redirect=no" title="Mechanical work">Mechanical work</a>)</span></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Mechanical work" redirects here. For other uses of "Work" in physics, see <a href="/wiki/Work_(electrical)" title="Work (electrical)">Work (electrical)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Work_(thermodynamics)" title="Work (thermodynamics)">Work (thermodynamics)</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-style:italic;">Work</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baseball_pitching_motion_2004.jpg"><img alt="Baseball pitching motion 2004.jpg" data-file-height="262" data-file-width="749" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Baseball_pitching_motion_2004.jpg/250px-Baseball_pitching_motion_2004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Baseball_pitching_motion_2004.jpg/375px-Baseball_pitching_motion_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Baseball_pitching_motion_2004.jpg/500px-Baseball_pitching_motion_2004.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div>A <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a> <a href="/wiki/Pitcher" title="Pitcher">pitcher</a> does positive work on the ball by applying a force to it over the distance it moves while in his grip.</div>
</td>
</tr>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;padding-top:0.15em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Classical_mechanics" title="Template:Classical mechanics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Classical_mechanics" title="Template talk:Classical mechanics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Classical_mechanics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> is said to do <b>work</b> if, when acting, there is a <a href="/wiki/Displacement_(vector)" title="Displacement (vector)">displacement</a> of the point of application in the direction of the force. For example, when a ball is held above the ground and then dropped, the work done on the ball as it falls is equal to the weight of the ball (a force) multiplied by the distance to the ground (a displacement).</p>
<p>Work transfers energy from one place to another or one form to another.</p>
<p>The term <i>work</i> was introduced in 1826 by the French mathematician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaspard-Gustave_Coriolis" title="Gaspard-Gustave Coriolis">Gaspard-Gustave Coriolis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> as "weight <i>lifted</i> through a height", which is based on the use of early <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a> to lift buckets of water out of flooded ore mines. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_unit" title="SI unit">SI unit</a> of work is the <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a> (J).</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Units">Units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The SI unit of work is the <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a> (J), which is defined as the work expended by a force of one <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newton</a> through a displacement of one <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a>.</p>
<p>The dimensionally equivalent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton-metre" title="Newton-metre">newton-metre</a> (N⋅m) is sometimes used as the measuring unit for work, but this can be confused with the unit newton-metre, which is the measurement unit of <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a>. Usage of N⋅m is discouraged by the <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">SI authority</a>, since it can lead to confusion as to whether the quantity expressed in newton metres is a torque measurement, or a measurement of work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Non-SI units of work include the <a href="/wiki/Erg" title="Erg">erg</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Foot-pound_(energy)" title="Foot-pound (energy)">foot-pound</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Foot-poundal" title="Foot-poundal">foot-poundal</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hour" title="Kilowatt hour">kilowatt hour</a>, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Litre-atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Litre-atmosphere (page does not exist)">litre-atmosphere</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">horsepower-hour</a>. Due to work having the same <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">physical dimension</a> as <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a>, occasionally measurement units typically reserved for heat or energy content, such as <a href="/wiki/Therm" title="Therm">therm</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BTU" title="BTU">BTU</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">Calorie</a>, are utilized as a measuring unit.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_and_energy">Work and energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Work and energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The work <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54a9c4c547f4d6111f81946cad242b18298d70b7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.446ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> done by a constant force of magnitude <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>

</dl>
<p>For example, if a force of 10 newtons (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="F" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/545fd099af8541605f7ee55f08225526be88ce57" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.751ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> = 10 N) acts along a point that travels 2 meters (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>s</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle s}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="s" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/01d131dfd7673938b947072a13a9744fe997e632" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.101ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> = 2 m), then it does the work <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>W</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54a9c4c547f4d6111f81946cad242b18298d70b7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.446ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> = (10 N)(2 m) = 20 N m = 20 J. This is approximately the work done lifting a 1 kg weight from ground level to over a person's head against the force of gravity. Notice that the work is doubled either by lifting twice the weight the same distance or by lifting the same weight twice the distance.</p>
<p>Work is closely related to <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>. The <a href="#Work-energy_principle">work-energy principle</a> states that an increase in the kinetic energy of a rigid body is caused by an equal amount of positive work done on the body by the resultant force acting on that body. Conversely, a decrease in kinetic energy is caused by an equal amount of negative work done by the resultant force.</p>
<p>From <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's second law</a>, it can be shown that work on a free (no fields), rigid (no internal degrees of freedom) body, is equal to the change in kinetic energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>K</mi>
<mi>E</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle KE}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="KE" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/507bb8592e58edbb55ba67f37ce998e58047489f" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.862ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> of the velocity and rotation of that body,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo>=</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>K</mi>
<mi>E</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W=\Delta KE.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W=\Delta KE." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fee3f13f31b6b8789817126396f1ee63342d4aae" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:12.021ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The work of forces generated by a potential function is known as <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> and the forces are said to be <a href="/wiki/Conservative_force" title="Conservative force">conservative</a>. Therefore, work on an object that is merely displaced in a conservative force <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">field</a>, without change in velocity or rotation, is equal to <i>minus</i> the change of potential energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
<mi>E</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle PE}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle PE}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3618f41498880a11110aadc8495b704cebacb831" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.542ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> of the object,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>W</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>P</mi>
<mi>E</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W=-\Delta PE.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W=-\Delta PE." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/747d29e1fce503ab050e247dcc5b606ef8e3a455" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:13.519ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>These formulas show that work is the energy associated with the action of a force, so work subsequently possesses the <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">physical dimensions</a>, and units, of energy. The work/energy principles discussed here are identical to Electric work/energy principles.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Constraint_forces">Constraint forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Constraint forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Constraint forces determine the movement of components in a system, constraining the object within a boundary (in the case of a slope plus gravity, the object is <i>stuck to</i> the slope, when attached to a taut string it cannot move in an outwards direction to make the string any 'tauter'). Constraint forces ensure the velocity in the direction of the constraint is zero, which means the constraint forces do not perform work on the system.</p>
<p>If the system <a href="/wiki/Virtual_work" title="Virtual work">doesn't change in time</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-4">[4]</a></sup> they eliminate all movement in the direction of the constraint, thus constraint forces do not perform work on the system, as the velocity of that object is constrained to be 0 parallel to this force, due to this force. This only applies for a single particle system. For example, in an <a href="/wiki/Atwood_machine" title="Atwood machine">Atwood machine</a>, the rope does work on each body, but keeping always the net virtual work null. There are, however, cases where this is not true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>For example, the <a href="/wiki/Centripetal_force" title="Centripetal force">centripetal force</a> exerted <i>inwards</i> by a string on a ball in uniform <a href="/wiki/Circular_motion" title="Circular motion">circular motion</a> <i>sideways</i> constrains the ball to circular motion restricting its movement away from the center of the circle. This force does zero work because it is perpendicular to the velocity of the ball.</p>
<p>Another example is a book on a table. If external forces are applied to the book so that it slides on the table, then the force exerted by the table constrains the book from moving downwards. The force exerted by the table supports the book and is perpendicular to its movement which means that this constraint force does not perform work.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_force" title="Magnetic force">magnetic force</a> on a charged particle is <span class="nowrap"><b>F</b> = <i>q</i><b>v</b> × <b>B</b></span>, where <i>q</i> is the charge, <b>v</b> is the velocity of the particle, and <b>B</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>. The result of a <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">cross product</a> is always perpendicular to both of the original vectors, so <span class="nowrap"><b>F</b> ⊥ <b>v</b></span>. The <a href="/wiki/Dot_product" title="Dot product">dot product</a> of two perpendicular vectors is always zero, so the work <span class="nowrap"><i>W</i> = <b>F</b> ⋅ <b>v</b> = 0</span>, and the magnetic force does not do work. It can change the direction of motion but never change the speed.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematical_calculation">Mathematical calculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mathematical calculation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For moving objects, the quantity of work/time (power) is calculated. Thus, at any instant, the rate of the work done by a force (measured in joules/second, or <b>watts</b>) is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scalar_product" title="Scalar product">scalar product</a> of the force (a vector), and the velocity vector of the point of application. This scalar product of force and velocity is classified as instantaneous <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a>. Just as velocities may be integrated over time to obtain a total distance, by the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_calculus" title="Fundamental theorem of calculus">fundamental theorem of calculus</a>, the total work along a path is similarly the time-integral of instantaneous power applied along the trajectory of the point of application.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Resnick_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Resnick-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Work is the result of a force on a point that moves through a displacement. As the point moves, it follows a curve <b>X</b>, with a velocity <b>v</b>, at each instant. The small amount of work <i>δW</i> that occurs over an instant of time <i>dt</i> is calculated as</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>δ<!-- δ --></mi>
<mi>W</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi>d</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">s</mi>

</mrow>
<mi>d</mi>
<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \delta W=\mathbf {F} \cdot d\mathbf {s} =\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {v} dt}</annotation>
</semantics>

</dl>
<p>where the <span class="nowrap"><b>F</b> ⋅ <b>v</b></span> is the power over the instant <i>dt</i>. The sum of these small amounts of work over the trajectory of the point yields the work,</p>

<p>where <i>C</i> is the trajectory from <b>x</b>(<i>t</i><sub>1</sub>) to <b>x</b>(<i>t</i><sub>2</sub>). This integral is computed along the trajectory of the particle, and is therefore said to be <i>path dependent</i>.</p>
<p>If the force is always directed along this line, and the magnitude of the force is <i>F</i>, then this integral simplifies to</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</dl>
<p>where <i>s</i> is the displacement of the point along the line.</p>
<p>This calculation can be generalized for a constant force that is not directed along the line, followed by the particle. In this case the <a href="/wiki/Dot_product" title="Dot product">dot product</a> <span class="nowrap"><b>F</b> ⋅ <i>d</i><b>s</b> = <i>F</i> cos <i>θ</i> <i>ds</i></span>, where <i>θ</i> is the angle between the force vector and the direction of movement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Resnick_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Resnick-5">[5]</a></sup> that is</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>W</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>C</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi>d</mi>

</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>

<mi>cos</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W=\int _{C}\mathbf {F} \cdot d\mathbf {s} =Fs\cos \theta .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W=\int _{C}\mathbf {F} \cdot d\mathbf {s} =Fs\cos \theta ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f14d27e0f49e195993daf06d71b65dfff36b10b5" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:26.044ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>In the notable case of a force applied to a body always at an angle of 90° from the velocity vector (as when a body moves in a circle under a central force), no work is done at all, since the cosine of 90 degrees is zero. Thus, no work can be performed by gravity on a planet with a circular orbit (this is ideal, as all orbits are slightly elliptical). Also, no work is done on a body moving circularly at a constant speed while constrained by mechanical force, such as moving at constant speed in a frictionless ideal centrifuge.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_done_by_a_variable_force">Work done by a variable force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Work done by a variable force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Calculating the work as "force times straight path segment" would only apply in the most simple of circumstances, as noted above. If force is changing, or if the body is moving along a curved path, possibly rotating and not necessarily rigid, then only the path of the application point of the force is relevant for the work done, and only the component of the force parallel to the application point <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> is doing work (positive work when in the same direction, and negative when in the opposite direction of the velocity). This component of force can be described by the scalar quantity called <i>scalar tangential component</i> (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="1">
<mi>F</mi>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \scriptstyle F\cos \theta }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\scriptstyle F\cos \theta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bd0bf1e4d180b03434005cea0b8122048dc90bc7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.013ex; height:1.843ex;"/></span>, where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="1">
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \scriptstyle \theta }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\scriptstyle \theta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/635d7bc2a3756a9c19ec7ba7f9aacc676c8a324a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.778ex; height:1.843ex;"/></span> is the angle between the force and the velocity). And then the most general definition of work can be formulated as follows:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>Work of a force is the line integral of its scalar tangential component along the path of its application point.</i></dd>
<dd>If the force varies (e.g. compressing a spring) we need to use calculus to find the work done. If the force is given by F(x) (a function of x) then the work done by the force along the x-axis from a to b is:</dd>
</dl>
<p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>W</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<munderover>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>a</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>b</mi>
</mrow>
</munderover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
<mo mathvariant="bold" stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi mathvariant="bold">s</mi>
<mo mathvariant="bold" stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi>d</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">s</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W=\int \limits _{a}^{b}\mathbf {F(s)} \cdot d\mathbf {s} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle W=\int \limits _{a}^{b}\mathbf {F(s)} \cdot d\mathbf {s} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3a41c982b440b74561c6432095517de862898b37" style="vertical-align: -4.005ex; width:16.987ex; height:9.343ex;"/></span></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Torque_and_rotation">Torque and rotation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Torque and rotation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Couple_(mechanics)" title="Couple (mechanics)">force couple</a> results from equal and opposite forces, acting on two different points of a rigid body. The sum (resultant) of these forces may cancel, but their effect on the body is the couple or torque <b>T</b>. The work of the torque is calculated as</p>

<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">T</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle dW=\mathbf {T} \cdot {\vec {\omega }}dt,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fe0423bca1161c6f79908e87b8e1930f53b9ad9" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:14.53ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where the <span class="nowrap"><b>T</b> ⋅ <b>ω</b></span> is the power over the instant <i>δt</i>. The sum of these small amounts of work over the trajectory of the rigid body yields the work,</p>
<dl>

</dl>
<p>This integral is computed along the trajectory of the rigid body with an angular velocity ω that varies with time, and is therefore said to be <i>path dependent</i>.</p>
<p>If the angular velocity vector maintains a constant direction, then it takes the form,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>where φ is the angle of rotation about the constant unit vector <b>S</b>. In this case, the work of the torque becomes,</p>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<p>This result can be understood more simply by considering the torque as arising from a force of constant magnitude <i>F</i>, being applied perpendicularly to a lever arm at a distance <i>r</i>, as shown in the figure. This force will act through the distance along the circular arc <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = <i>rφ</i></span>, so the work done is</p>

</dl>
<p>Introduce the torque <span class="nowrap"><i>τ</i> = <i>Fr</i></span>, to obtain</p>

<p>as presented above.</p>
<p>Notice that only the component of torque in the direction of the angular velocity vector contributes to the work.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_and_potential_energy">Work and potential energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Work and potential energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The scalar product of a force <b>F</b> and the velocity <b>v</b> of its point of application defines the <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a> input to a system at an instant of time. Integration of this power over the trajectory of the point of application, <span class="nowrap"><i>C</i> = <b>x</b>(<i>t</i>)</span>, defines the work input to the system by the force.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Path_dependence">Path dependence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Path dependence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Therefore, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_work" title="Mechanical work">work</a> done by a force <b>F</b> on an object that travels along a curve <i>C</i> is given by the <a href="/wiki/Line_integral" title="Line integral">line integral</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>W</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>C</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi>d</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">x</mi>
</mrow>

<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W=\int _{C}\mathbf {F} \cdot d\mathbf {x} =\int _{t_{1}}^{t_{2}}\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {v} dt,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W=\int _{C}\mathbf {F} \cdot d\mathbf {x} =\int _{t_{1}}^{t_{2}}\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {v} dt," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ad41ea906a31472351d54b4d3a6c1644fef82757" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:29.943ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>dx</i>(<i>t</i>) defines the trajectory <i>C</i> and <b>v</b> is the velocity along this trajectory. In general this integral requires the path along which the velocity is defined, so the evaluation of work is said to be path dependent.</p>
<p>The time derivative of the integral for work yields the instantaneous power,</p>
<dl>

<mo>=</mo>
<mi>P</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>

</mrow>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">v</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {dW}{dt}}=P(t)=\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {v} .}</annotation>
</semantics>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Path_independence">Path independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Path independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If the work for an applied force is independent of the path, then the work done by the force is, by the <a href="/wiki/Gradient_theorem" title="Gradient theorem">gradient theorem</a>, the potential function evaluated at the start and end of the trajectory of the point of application. Such a force is said to be <a href="/wiki/Conservative_force" title="Conservative force">conservative</a>. This means that there is a potential function <i>U</i>(<b>x</b>), that can be evaluated at the two points <b>x</b>(<i>t</i><sub>1</sub>) and <b>x</b>(<i>t</i><sub>2</sub>) to obtain the work over any trajectory between these two points. It is tradition to define this function with a negative sign so that positive work is a reduction in the potential, that is</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>W</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>C</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W=\int _{C}\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} =\int _{\mathbf {x} (t_{1})}^{\mathbf {x} (t_{2})}\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} =U(\mathbf {x} (t_{1}))-U(\mathbf {x} (t_{2})).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W=\int _{C}\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} =\int _{\mathbf {x} (t_{1})}^{\mathbf {x} (t_{2})}\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathrm {d} \mathbf {x} =U(\mathbf {x} (t_{1}))-U(\mathbf {x} (t_{2}))." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c2f561642e3b3969b5675183d8bd1c571bebf0b3" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:55.078ex; height:6.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The function <i>U</i>(<b>x</b>) is called the <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> associated with the applied force. Examples of forces that have potential energies are gravity and spring forces.</p>
<p>In this case, the <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradient</a> of work yields</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∂<!-- ∂ --></mi>
<mi>U</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∂<!-- ∂ --></mi>
<mi>x</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>

</mrow>
<mrow>

</mfrac>

<mo>,</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>

<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∂<!-- ∂ --></mi>

</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \nabla W=-\nabla U=-\left({\frac {\partial U}{\partial x}},{\frac {\partial U}{\partial y}},{\frac {\partial U}{\partial z}}\right)=\mathbf {F} ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \nabla W=-\nabla U=-\left({\frac {\partial U}{\partial x}},{\frac {\partial U}{\partial y}},{\frac {\partial U}{\partial z}}\right)=\mathbf {F} ,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/992dd2e43776610739af8c23ec67b3856c2e8d4c" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:41.237ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>and the force <b>F</b> is said to be "derivable from a potential."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because the potential <i>U</i> defines a force <b>F</b> at every point <b>x</b> in space, the set of forces is called a <a href="/wiki/Force_field_(physics)" title="Force field (physics)">force field</a>. The power applied to a body by a force field is obtained from the gradient of the work, or potential, in the direction of the velocity <b>V</b> of the body, that is</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∇<!-- ∇ --></mi>
<mi>U</mi>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">v</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">v</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P(t)=-\nabla U\cdot \mathbf {v} =\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {v} .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle P(t)=-\nabla U\cdot \mathbf {v} =\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {v} .}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/39cdfc586392f80d5ef3b7de7d2989289b475e9e" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:24.784ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_by_gravity">Work by gravity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Work by gravity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:170px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Work_of_gravity_F_dot_d_equals_mgh.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="138" data-file-width="168" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/Work_of_gravity_F_dot_d_equals_mgh.JPG" width="168"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Work_of_gravity_F_dot_d_equals_mgh.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gravity <span class="nowrap"><i>F</i> = <i>mg</i></span> does work <span class="nowrap"><i>W</i> = <i>mgh</i></span> along any descending path</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the absence of other forces, gravity results in a constant downward acceleration of every freely moving object. Near Earth's surface the acceleration due to gravity is <span class="nowrap"><i>g</i> = 9.8 m⋅s<sup>−2</sup></span> and the gravitational force on an object of mass <i>m</i> is <span class="nowrap"><b>F</b><sub>g</sub> = <i>mg</i></span>. It is convenient to imagine this gravitational force concentrated at the center of mass of the object.</p>
<p>If an object is displaced upwards or downwards a vertical distance <span class="nowrap"><i>y</i><sub>2</sub> − <i>y</i><sub>1</sub></span>, the work <i>W</i> done on the object by its weight mg is:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>W</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>g</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msub>
<mi>y</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>y</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>g</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mi>g</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>y</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W=F_{g}(y_{2}-y_{1})=F_{g}\Delta y=-mg\Delta y}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W=F_{g}(y_{2}-y_{1})=F_{g}\Delta y=-mg\Delta y" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b3cdc03e07f1c8155b2bee2103f65a004f0f79ed" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:37.164ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>F<sub>g</sub></i> is weight (pounds in imperial units, and newtons in SI units), and Δ<i>y</i> is the change in height <i>y</i>. Notice that the work done by gravity depends only on the vertical movement of the object. The presence of friction does not affect the work done on the object by its weight.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_by_gravity_in_space">Work by gravity in space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Work by gravity in space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</msup>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {d}{dt}}r^{-1}=-r^{-2}{\dot {r}}=-{\frac {\dot {r}}{r^{2}}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\frac {d}{dt}}r^{-1}=-r^{-2}{\dot {r}}=-{\frac {\dot {r}}{r^{2}}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68f74984cb177d2d1be704437e3b93467b37cf36" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:24.497ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The function</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>U</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>G</mi>
<mi>M</mi>
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
<mi>r</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle U=-{\frac {GMm}{r}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="U=-{\frac {GMm}{r}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e4ba4c6266dadc7bda4935bf42846bd225e4ed1" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:14.555ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>is the gravitational potential function, also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential_energy" title="Gravitational potential energy">gravitational potential energy</a>. The negative sign follows the convention that work is gained from a loss of potential energy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_by_a_spring">Work by a spring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Work by a spring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Analogie_ressorts_contrainte.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="429" data-file-width="278" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Analogie_ressorts_contrainte.svg/170px-Analogie_ressorts_contrainte.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Analogie_ressorts_contrainte.svg/255px-Analogie_ressorts_contrainte.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Analogie_ressorts_contrainte.svg/340px-Analogie_ressorts_contrainte.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Analogie_ressorts_contrainte.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Forces in springs assembled in parallel</div>

<p>Consider a spring that exerts a horizontal force <span class="nowrap"><b>F</b> = (−<i>kx</i>, 0, 0)</span> that is proportional to its deflection in the <i>x</i> direction independent of how a body moves. The work of this spring on a body moving along the space with the curve <span class="nowrap"><b>X</b>(<i>t</i>) = (<i>x</i>(<i>t</i>), <i>y</i>(<i>t</i>), <i>z</i>(<i>t</i>))</span>, is calculated using its velocity, <span class="nowrap"><b>v</b> = (<i>v</i><sub>x</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>y</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>z</sub>)</span>, to obtain</p>

<p>For convenience, consider contact with the spring occurs at <span class="nowrap"><i>t</i> = 0</span>, then the integral of the product of the distance <i>x</i> and the x-velocity, <i>xv</i><sub>x</sub>, is (1/2)<i>x</i><sup>2</sup>.</p>

<p>Where <i>P</i> is pressure, <i>V</i> is volume, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are initial and final volumes.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work.E2.80.93energy_principle">Work–energy principle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Work–energy principle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The principle of work and <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> (also known as the <b>work–energy principle</b>) states that <i>the work done by all forces acting on a particle (the work of the resultant force) equals the change in the kinetic energy of the particle.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> That is, the work <i>W</i> done by the <a href="/wiki/Resultant_force" title="Resultant force">resultant force</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Particle" title="Particle">particle</a> equals the change in the particle's kinetic energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>E</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>k</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E_{k}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="E_{k}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7587849b44d775263271e89499f4327eeac5dc81" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.822ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Young-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mrow>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mi>m</mi>
<msubsup>
<mi>v</mi>

<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msubsup>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W=\Delta E_{k}={\tfrac {1}{2}}mv_{2}^{2}-{\tfrac {1}{2}}mv_{1}^{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W=\Delta E_{k}={\tfrac {1}{2}}mv_{2}^{2}-{\tfrac {1}{2}}mv_{1}^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/510f35d37edc1d6ea060662e08ac342c9d4fd58a" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:28.115ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span>,</dd>
</dl>
<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v_{1}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/98d33f5d498d528bd8c10edc8ac8c34347f32b3a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.2ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v_{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eb04c423c2cb809c30cac725befa14ffbf4c85f3" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.2ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> are the <a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">speeds</a> of the particle before and after the work is done and <i>m</i> is its <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>.</p>
<p>The derivation of the <i>work–energy principle</i> begins with Newton's second law and the resultant force on a particle which includes forces applied to the particle and constraint forces imposed on its movement. Computation of the scalar product of the forces with the velocity of the particle evaluates the instantaneous power added to the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Constraints define the direction of movement of the particle by ensuring there is no component of velocity in the direction of the constraint force. This also means the constraint forces do not add to the instantaneous power. The time integral of this scalar equation yields work from the instantaneous power, and kinetic energy from the scalar product of velocity and acceleration. The fact the work–energy principle eliminates the constraint forces underlies <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_mechanics" title="Lagrangian mechanics">Lagrangian mechanics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>This section focuses on the work–energy principle as it applies to particle dynamics. In more general systems work can change the <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> of a mechanical device, the thermal energy in a thermal system, or the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_energy" title="Electrical energy">electrical energy</a> in an electrical device. Work transfers energy from one place to another or one form to another.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derivation_for_a_particle_moving_along_a_straight_line">Derivation for a particle moving along a straight line</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Derivation for a particle moving along a straight line">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the case the <a href="/wiki/Resultant_force" title="Resultant force">resultant force</a> <b>F</b> is constant in both magnitude and direction, and parallel to the velocity of the particle, the particle is moving with constant acceleration <i>a</i> along a straight line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The relation between the net force and the acceleration is given by the equation <i>F</i> = <i>ma</i> (<a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion#Newton.27s_second_law" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's second law</a>), and the particle <a href="/wiki/Displacement_(vector)" title="Displacement (vector)">displacement</a> <i>s</i> can be expressed by the equation</p>

<p>Vertical displacement derivation</p>
<p><i>W = F × S = mg × h</i></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_derivation_of_the_work.E2.80.93energy_theorem_for_a_particle">General derivation of the work–energy theorem for a particle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: General derivation of the work–energy theorem for a particle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For any net force acting on a particle moving along any curvilinear path, it can be demonstrated that its work equals the change in the kinetic energy of the particle by a simple derivation analogous to the equation above. Some authors call this result <i>work–energy principle</i>, but it is more widely known as <b>the work–energy theorem</b>:</p>

<p>The identity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

From the identity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>The remaining part of the above derivation is just simple calculus, same as in the preceding rectilinear case.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derivation_for_a_particle_in_constrained_movement">Derivation for a particle in constrained movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Derivation for a particle in constrained movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In particle dynamics, a formula equating work applied to a system to its change in kinetic energy is obtained as a first integral of <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's second law of motion</a>. It is useful to notice that the resultant force used in Newton's laws can be separated into forces that are applied to the particle and forces imposed by constraints on the movement of the particle. Remarkably, the work of a constraint force is zero, therefore only the work of the applied forces need be considered in the work–energy principle.</p>
<p>To see this, consider a particle P that follows the trajectory <b>X</b>(<i>t</i>) with a force <b>F</b> acting on it. Isolate the particle from its environment to expose constraint forces <b>R</b>, then Newton's Law takes the form</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">X</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>¨<!-- ¨ --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} +\mathbf {R} =m{\ddot {\mathbf {X} }},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbf {F} +\mathbf {R} =m{\ddot {\mathbf {X} }}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8c62f7a4f5084fa2fc19729d1067f80bf0bc2957" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:14.405ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>m</i> is the mass of the particle.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vector_formulation">Vector formulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Vector formulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Note that n dots above a vector indicates its nth <a href="/wiki/Time_derivative" title="Time derivative">time derivative</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scalar_product" title="Scalar product">scalar product</a> of each side of Newton's law with the velocity vector yields</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tangential_and_normal_components">Tangential and normal components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Tangential and normal components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<p>The result is the work–energy principle for particle dynamics,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi>K</mi>
<mo>.</mo>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W=\Delta K.\!}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W=\Delta K.\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0c5e2034de5cba2d6c7db6bd5e7cfac4463ffcda" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; margin-right: -0.204ex; width:10.051ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This derivation can be generalized to arbitrary rigid body systems.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moving_in_a_straight_line_.28skid_to_a_stop.29">Moving in a straight line (skid to a stop)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Moving in a straight line (skid to a stop)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Consider the case of a vehicle moving along a straight horizontal trajectory under the action of a driving force and gravity that sum to <b>F</b>. The constraint forces between the vehicle and the road define <b>R</b>, and we have</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">X</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>¨<!-- ¨ --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} +\mathbf {R} =m{\ddot {\mathbf {X} }}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbf {F} +\mathbf {R} =m{\ddot {\mathbf {X} }}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1e89fbfe7c74133802580327c00a2a78bc96945" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:14.405ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>For convenience let the trajectory be along the X-axis, so <span class="nowrap"><b>X</b> = (<i>d</i>, 0)</span> and the velocity is <span class="nowrap"><b>V</b> = (<i>v</i>, 0)</span>, then <span class="nowrap"><b>R</b> ⋅ <b>V</b> = 0</span>, and <span class="nowrap"><b>F</b> ⋅ <b>V</b> = <i>F</i><sub>x</sub><i>v</i></span>, where <i>F</i><sub>x</sub> is the component of <b>F</b> along the X-axis, so</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>x</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>˙<!-- ˙ --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F_{x}v=m{\dot {v}}v.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="F_{x}v=m{\dot {v}}v." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/98d67ac1d0acb5f16e2cd5761b74780ded8ea771" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:12.016ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Integration of both sides yields</p>
<dl>

<p>If F<sub>x</sub> is constant along the trajectory, then the integral of velocity is distance, so</p>

<p>As an example consider a car skidding to a stop, where <i>k</i> is the coefficient of friction and <i>W</i> is the weight of the car. Then the force along the trajectory is <span class="nowrap"><i>F</i><sub>x</sub> = −<i>kW</i></span>. The velocity <i>v</i> of the car can be determined from the length <i>s</i> of the skid using the work–energy principle,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle kWs={\frac {W}{2g}}v^{2},\quad {\mbox{or}}\quad v={\sqrt {2ksg}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="kWs={\frac {W}{2g}}v^{2},\quad {\mbox{or}}\quad v={\sqrt {2ksg}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6671f07081ccf7f7cbcd9c5f1d4a385dde7d2556" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:33.004ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Notice that this formula uses the fact that the mass of the vehicle is <span class="nowrap"><i>m</i> = <i>W</i>/<i>g</i></span>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coasting_down_a_mountain_road_.28gravity_racing.29">Coasting down a mountain road (gravity racing)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Coasting down a mountain road (gravity racing)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Consider the case of a vehicle that starts at rest and coasts down a mountain road, the work-energy principle helps compute the minimum distance that the vehicle travels to reach a velocity <i>V</i>, of say 60 mph (88 fps). Rolling resistance and air drag will slow the vehicle down so the actual distance will be greater than if these forces are neglected.</p>
<p>Let the trajectory of the vehicle following the road be <b>X</b>(<i>t</i>) which is a curve in three-dimensional space. The force acting on the vehicle that pushes it down the road is the constant force of gravity <span class="nowrap"><b>F</b> = (0, 0, <i>W</i>)</span>, while the force of the road on the vehicle is the constraint force <b>R</b>. Newton's second law yields,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">X</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>¨<!-- ¨ --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} +\mathbf {R} =m{\ddot {\mathbf {X} }}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbf {F} +\mathbf {R} =m{\ddot {\mathbf {X} }}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1e89fbfe7c74133802580327c00a2a78bc96945" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:14.405ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scalar_product" title="Scalar product">scalar product</a> of this equation with the velocity, <span class="nowrap"><b>V</b> = (<i>v</i><sub>x</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>y</sub>, <i>v</i><sub>z</sub>)</span>, yields</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>z</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>V</mi>
<mo>˙<!-- ˙ --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mi>V</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Wv_{z}=m{\dot {V}}V,}</annotation>
</semantics>

</dl>
<p>where <i>V</i> is the magnitude of <b>V</b>. The constraint forces between the vehicle and the road cancel from this equation because <span class="nowrap"><b>R</b> ⋅ <b>V</b> = 0</span>, which means they do no work. Integrate both sides to obtain</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<msub>
<mi>t</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msub>
<mi>t</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>

<mi>W</mi>
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>z</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mi>d</mi>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>m</mi>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<msup>
<mi>V</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>

<msub>
<mi>t</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</msup>

<msub>
<mi>t</mi>

<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>

</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \int _{t_{1}}^{t_{2}}Wv_{z}dt={\frac {m}{2}}V^{2}(t_{2})-{\frac {m}{2}}V^{2}(t_{1}).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\int _{t_{1}}^{t_{2}}Wv_{z}dt={\frac {m}{2}}V^{2}(t_{2})-{\frac {m}{2}}V^{2}(t_{1})." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f0c097ed1b0e1b8375cbc4f49c292bbec2a7d91d" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:37.055ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The weight force <i>W</i> is constant along the trajectory and the integral of the vertical velocity is the vertical distance, therefore,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi>z</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>m</mi>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<msup>
<mi>V</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W\Delta z={\frac {m}{2}}V^{2}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="W\Delta z={\frac {m}{2}}V^{2}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/564ac47e1f80ccbff880054fe66b9c2a8a1639f9" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:15.133ex; height:4.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>Recall that V(t<sub>1</sub>)=0. Notice that this result does not depend on the shape of the road followed by the vehicle.</p>
<p>In order to determine the distance along the road assume the downgrade is 6%, which is a steep road. This means the altitude decreases 6 feet for every 100 feet traveled—for angles this small the sin and tan functions are approximately equal. Therefore, the distance <i>s</i> in feet down a 6% grade to reach the velocity <i>V</i> is at least</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>s</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>z</mi>
</mrow>
<mn>0.06</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>8.3</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<msup>
<mi>V</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mi>g</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
<mspace width="1em"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>or</mtext>
</mrow>
<mspace width="1em"></mspace>
<mi>s</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>8.3</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<msup>
<mn>88</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mn>32.2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<mn>2000</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>ft</mtext>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle s={\frac {\Delta z}{0.06}}=8.3{\frac {V^{2}}{g}},\quad {\mbox{or}}\quad s=8.3{\frac {88^{2}}{32.2}}\approx 2000{\mbox{ft}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="s={\frac {\Delta z}{0.06}}=8.3{\frac {V^{2}}{g}},\quad {\mbox{or}}\quad s=8.3{\frac {88^{2}}{32.2}}\approx 2000{\mbox{ft}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ef15d13d5d1554064afb40285f44f0a2b87d3028" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:49.264ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This formula uses the fact that the weight of the vehicle is <span class="nowrap"><i>W</i> = <i>mg</i></span>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_of_forces_acting_on_a_rigid_body">Work of forces acting on a rigid body</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Work_(physics)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Work of forces acting on a rigid body">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The work of forces acting at various points on a single rigid body can be calculated from the work of a <a href="/wiki/Resultant_force" title="Resultant force">resultant force and torque</a>. To see this, let the forces <b>F</b><sub>1</sub>, <b>F</b><sub>2</sub> ... <b>F</b><sub>n</sub> act on the points <b>X</b><sub>1</sub>, <b>X</b><sub>2</sub> ... <b>X</b><sub><i>n</i></sub> in a rigid body.</p>
<p>The trajectories of <b>X</b><sub><i>i</i></sub>, <i>i</i> = 1, ..., <i>n</i> are defined by the movement of the rigid body. This movement is given by the set of rotations [<i>A</i>(<i>t</i>)] and the trajectory <b>d</b>(<i>t</i>) of a reference point in the body. Let the coordinates <b>x</b><sub><i>i</i></sub> <i>i</i> = 1, ..., <i>n</i> define these points in the moving rigid body's <a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system" title="Cartesian coordinate system">reference frame</a> <i>M</i>, so that the trajectories traced in the fixed frame <i>F</i> are given by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
